For Portuguese databases, the search terms applied were any (“terapia de aceitação e compromisso”) AND title (“valores”, “sentido”, “propósito”). A search in English was conducted in all databases using search terms any (“acceptance and commitment therapy” OR “acceptance-based behavioral therapy”) AND title (“valu”, “meaning”, [...] ACT defines values as “freely chosen, verbally constructed consequences of ongoing, dynamic, evolving patterns of activity, which establish predominant reinforcers for that activity that are intrinsic in engagement in the valued behavioral pattern itself” (Wilson & DuFrene, 2009, p. 66). The behavior of valuing is an example of verbal behavior and is understood in ACT through the lens of Relational Frame Theory (RFT). This functional analytic theory of language and cognition seeks to understand [...] The investigated values interventions are an illustration of the challenges in isolating the functional processes of the psychological flexibility model. For example, acceptance and defusion are usually addressed in ACT interventions as a mean to values engagement, therefore it is not straight forward how to work with values “standalone”. Although the measures employed by the studies aimed at evaluating the effects of each component in the outcome variable, some interventions targeted multiple\nRaw Content: International Journal of Psychology and Psychological Therapy, 2020, 20, 3, 355-372 20th ANNIVERSARY EDITION // EDICIÓN XX ANIVERSARIO Printed in Spain. All rights reserved.\tCopyright © 2020 AAC A Systematic Review of Values Interventions in Acceptance and Commitment Therapy Gustavo Matheus Rahal* Instituto Federal do Paraná, Brasil Marcia Cristina Caserta Gon Universidade Estadual de Londrina, Brasil * Correspondence: Gustavo Matheus Rahal, Instituto Federal do Paraná, Foz do Iguaçu/PR, Brazil. Email: grahal@me.com Abstract Acceptance and Commitment Therapy (ACT) is characterized by a focus on contextual change and advances topics like acceptance, mindfulness, values, spirituality and relationship. It sets itself apart from other third wave approaches by bringing to the center of the therapeutic work values clarification and living. Previous systematic reviews provided support for the efficacy and effectiveness of Acceptance and Commitment Therapy (ACT) but it’s unknown of reviews that comprehensively assess values interventions with a focus on examining the specific effects of working with values or meaning in life in a wide variety of settings, populations and methodological designs. The goal of this study is to investigate empirical interventions that utilizes values as conceptualized by ACT. Systematic searches in 5 databases were performed up to April 2020. For inclusion, the intervention study must have targeted values process as conceptualized by ACT. Systematic reviews, theoretical or conceptual papers were excluded. Analyzed data were publication year, language, country, number and description of participants, mean age, sample origin, outcome variables, study design and quality and overall results. Seventeen studies were identified. Results show a broad scope of research methods, in a variety of settings and populations. According to reported results, values interventions had the desired effect on the outcome variable.\nKeywords: Acceptance and Commitment Therapy, values, meaning in life, Relational Frame Theory, review.\nHow to cite this paper: Matheus Rahal G & Caserta Gon MC (2020). A Systematic Review of Values Interventions in Acceptance and Commitment Therapy. International Journal of Psychology & Psychological Therapy, 20, 3, 355-372.\nAcceptance and Commitment Therapy (ACT) is part of the third-wave of behavioral therapies, characterized by a focus on contextual change, an emphasis of function over form and an openness to areas traditionally reserved for the less empirical wings of clinical intervention (Hayes, 2004). It also advances topics like acceptance, mindfulness, cognitive defusion, dialectics, values, spirituality and relationship (Hayes, 2004). ACT sets itself apart from other third-wave approaches by bringing to the center of the therapeutic work values clarification and living (Hayes, Strosahl, & Wilson, 2012).\nNovelty and Significance What is already known about the topic?\n• Previous reviews provide support for the efficacy of ACT across a range of conditions. • Laboratory-based studies indicate that when employing values component of ACT plus mindfulness the effect on outcome variables is large.\nWhat this paper adds?\n• The review found a relevant number of chronic pain studies (35%) and results show that values have a significant effect on targeted variables.\n• Values-based interventions for chronic pain enhance life engagement and pain tolerance.\n• Data collection of the analyzed studies was heavily based on self-report measures. Direct observation of behavior was present in only one study.\n356 © International Journal of Psychology & Psychological Therapy, 2020, 20, 3 https://www. ijpsy. com Matheus Rahal & Caserta Gon Values play a central role in ACT. According to Hayes, Strosahl, and Wilson (2012), “all ACT techniques are eventually subordinated to helping the client live in accord with his or her chosen values” (p. 322). Differently from symptom reduction psychotherapy’s, valued living is the primary treatment outcome of ACT (Wilson, Sandoz, Kitchens, & Roberts, 2010). The goal is to generate the conditions for the client to behave according to personal values while being willing to notice the aversive private events that show up as a consequence of living.\nACT defines values as “freely chosen, verbally constructed consequences of ongoing, dynamic, evolving patterns of activity, which establish predominant reinforcers for that activity that are intrinsic in engagement in the valued behavioral pattern itself” (Wilson & DuFrene, 2009, p. 66). The behavior of valuing is an example of verbal behavior and is understood in ACT through the lens of Relational Frame Theory (RFT). This functional analytic theory of language and cognition seeks to understand and influence language phenomena through the study of derived stimulus relations and arbitrary applicable relational responding (Hayes, Barnes-Holmes, & Roche, 2001).\nValues research can contribute to understand change processes in psychotherapy, not only limited to ACT. For example, Gloster, Klotsche, Ciarrochi, Eifert, Sonntag, Wittchen, and Hoyer (2017) shows that engagement in valued behaviors precedes reductions in suffering. This finding implies that part of the reason a client is suffering in the first place is due to a disconnect with his/her own personal values (Gloster et alia, 2017). Pain induced laboratory studies also point to the importance of values articulation as strengthening the willingness to act in the presence of pain/suffering (Páez, Luciano, Gutiérrez, Valdivia, Ortega, & Rodríguez, 2008; Páez, Luciano, Gutiérrez, Valdivia, Rodríguez, & Ortega, 2008).\nPrevious systematic reviews provided support for the efficacy and effectiveness of ACT across a range of conditions including psychosis (Wakefield, Roebuck, & Boyden, 2018), anxiety disorders (García & Valdivia, 2018; Soo, Tate, & Lane-Brown, 2011; Swain, Hancock, Hainsworth, & Bowman, 2013), body image (Griffiths, Williamson, Zucchelli, Paraskeva, & Moss, 2018), chronic pain (Feliu Soler, Montesinos, Gutiérrez, Scott, McCracken, & Luciano, 2018; Hann & McCracken, 2014; Hughes, Clark, Colclough, Dale, & McMillan, 2017), chronic diseases and long-term conditions (Graham, Gouick, Krahé, & Gillanders, 2016). There are also systematic review that evaluate different methods of delivery, including web-delivery (Brown, Glendenning, Hoon, & John, 2016) and self-help (French, Golijani-Moghaddam, & Schröder, 2017). Meta-analysis of ACT effectiveness and its empirical support look at the results of the complete intervention package (c.f. Feliu Soler et alia, 2018; Ruiz, 2010; Smout, Hayes, Atkins, Klausen, & Duguid, 2012).\nLevin, Hildebrandt, Lillis, and Hayes (2012) evaluated the effect of various ACT model components individually, but limits itself to laboratory-based studies. The results indicate that when comparing values component with inactive control, the impact of the various studies on primary outcome was small (Cohen d= .41, N= 5) but when combined with mindfulness interventions it was large (d= 1.37, N= 5; mindfulness alone d=.46, N= 8). An example of values only laboratory intervention was writing about personally relevant values. For values plus mindful, interventions included metaphors like “Tug-of-war with a monster” and “Chinese finger trap” (cf. Stoddard & Afari, 2014). Imagery exercises linked endurance of pain for the purpose of previously identified personal value. In overall, values plus mindful interventions created a value-oriented context that encouraged the participants to continue with the task despite the exposure to pain or discomfort.\nhttps://www. ijpsy. com © International Journal of Psychology & Psychological Therapy, 2020, 20, 3 Values Interventions in ACT 357 Another study worth mentioning is Stockton, Kellett, Berrios, Sirois, Wilkinson, & Miles (2019), a systematic review of the mediational impact of ACT components. Only one study that examined values as a mediational process was referred to. To this date is unknown of reviews that comprehensively assess values interventions with a focus on examining the specific effects of working with values or meaning in life in a wide variety of settings, populations and methodological designs.\nThe goal of this work is to analyze empirical intervention studies that investigate the effects of ACT values component. To achieve this end, it was carried out a systematic review of interventions that used values component of ACT. Method This review was based on Manchado Garabito, Tamames Gómez, López González, Mohedano Macías, D’Agostino, & Veiga de Cabo (2009) exploratory systematic review protocol, that aims to explore a certain field by reviewing multiple studies and interventions types. The protocol is composed of the following steps: 1) definition of inclusion and exclusion criteria, 2) identification of data sources and search strategies, 3) selection and classification of studies, 4) choice of variables to be assessed, 5) data analysis.\nFor inclusion in this study the articles needed to be published in a peer-reviewed journal in Portuguese or English. Unpublished dissertations or thesis were excluded. For inclusion, the study must have targeted values process as conceptualized by ACT, and if other processes were also targeted, i.e. the intervention delivered all ACT components, results must show the moderating/mediational effect of values alone in the overall treatment result. Systematic reviews, theoretical or conceptual papers were excluded.\nA systematic search for articles on values interventions was conducted up to April 2020 on 5 electronic databases (IndexPSI, PePSIC, PsycINFO, Scielo and WebOfScience). For Portuguese databases, the search terms applied were any (“terapia de aceitação e compromisso”) AND title (“valores”, “sentido”, “propósito”). A search in English was conducted in all databases using search terms any (“acceptance and commitment therapy” OR “acceptance-based behavioral therapy”) AND title (“valu*”, “meaning”, “purpose”). No date restrictions were applied. Acceptance-based Behavioral Therapy developed by Roemer & Orsillo (2014) became part of the search after showing up in the initial screening. This approach also uses values as conceptualized by ACT.\nThe selection of studies occurred according to the defined inclusion and exclusion criteria. After removal of duplicates and screening abstracts of the remaining studies, full-text articles were retrieved for examination.\nThe extracted data of included studies were publication year, language, country, number and description of participants, mean age, percent female, sample origin (e.g. clinical vs. non-clinical sample), outcome variables (e.g. measure of psychological symptoms), study design (e.g. mediational analysis, laboratory-based), quality (e.g. randomized, controlled, follow-up) and overall results.\nThe data was organized in Mendeley, taking advantage of its ta"

        "text": "Detailed Results:\n\nTitle: 3 ways to understand what Self is in IFS (Internal Family Systems ...\nURL: https://www.therapywithalessio.com/articles/what-is-self-and-self-energy-in-ifs-internal-family-systems-therapy\nContent: Self and Self-energy are key concepts to IFS (Internal Family Systems) Therapy because they describe the healing principle that underpins the whole method.\nRaw Content: # [Therapy with Alessio](/)\n\n![Therapy with Alessio](//images.squarespace-cdn.com/content/v1/5cd007b4348cd92afabde0b1/1557143906338-3BWFLIOR8ZXVX7KIOUDB/Logo_Alessio+%28Custom%29.png?format=1500w)\n\nYour Custom Text Here\n\n# [Therapy with Alessio](/)\n\n![Therapy with Alessio](//images.squarespace-cdn.com/content/v1/5cd007b4348cd92afabde0b1/1557143906338-3BWFLIOR8ZXVX7KIOUDB/Logo_Alessio+%28Custom%29.png?format=1500w)\n\n# [3 ways to understand what Self is in IFS (Internal Family Systems) Therapy](/articles/what-is-self-and-self-energy-in-ifs-internal-family-systems-therapy)\n\n![](https://images.squarespace-cdn.com/content/v1/5cd007b4348cd92afabde0b1/1612955792689-PZCQY02GSPT6E36GBX20/love-1672154_1920.jpg)\n\nLearn about Self in IFS\n\nSelf and Self-energy are key concepts to IFS (Internal Family Systems) Therapy because they describe the healing principle that underpins the whole method.\n\nPracticing IFS Therapy without engaging with Self or Self-energy is like trying to eat ice cream without dealing with the fact that ice cream is cold. Without cold, there is no ice cream… and without Self, there is no healing in IFS. I am going to answer a few questions about Self and Self-energy.\n\n## What is the difference between Self and Self-energy?\n\nSelf is a state of consciousness from which is possible to heal mental health issues. When we are in “Self”, we experience (some of) the so-called 8 C’s, which are *Compassion, Curiosity, Calm, Clarity, Courage, Connectedness, Confidence, and Creativity*. There are 5 further attributes to Self, which are knows as the 5 P’s, and they are *Playfulness, Patience, Presence, Perspective, and Persistence*.\n\nReaders who have good memory might find it easy to remember all these words, but it is not necessary to be able to list them or learn them by heart. For example, if you are on the dyslexic spectrum like me, you might want to rely on the **experience of being in Self** without using words. I call this the “embodied experience of Self”.\n\n### The embodied experience of Self\n\nI use the word “embodied” because Self is not just an idea, but it is a state of being that includes a state of mind, feelings and body. When you are in Self, you can experience the following in your body\n\nA sense of expansion in your chest area\n\nThe body feels lighter\n\nThe head feels less crowded and thinking is more clear and peaceful\n\nBreathing is smooth and deeper than usual\n\nFace muscles are relaxed\n\nAt times, if there is a lot of stress in the system, feelings of tiredness are felt when in Self.\n\nIt is now time to describe what is Self-energy. Self-energy is basically the experience that goes with the state of Self. “Energy” is a much wider word than “experience” and describes what it feels to be in Self, the “vibe” that a person feels and radiates when a person is in Self. IFS therapists constantly encourage the expansion of Self-energy and try to understand how much Self-energy is available to the client at any given moment of therapy.\n\nThe ides of Self-energy is more flexible than Self because some parts of the system can hold various degrees of Self-energy even if these parts are not Self. We can describe the process of healing through IFS as the process of allowing all parts to operate with as much Self-energy as possible.\n\n## Why is Self so important in IFS?\n\nSelf is key to IFS Therapy because it is at the base of its healing principle and it is also important experience clients have during an effective IFS session.\n\nThe healing principle of IFS Therapy is the following:\n\nHealing happens when Self interacts with parts. The stronger the relationship between Self and parts is, the more healing will spontaneously happen\n\nThe aim of each IFS Therapy session is for the client to identify parts, enter the state of Self, and, from that state, communicate to the parts.\n\nThe more empathic, compassionate, open and calm the communication between Self and parts is, the more healing happens. It is through this dialogue that parts heal from traumas, let go of dysfunctional behaviours and of distorted views of reality.\n\n## 3 ways to describe Self in IFS\n\nSelf can be described in various ways. The reader is free to choose whatever way suits best their belief system. I am going to present here three definitions, which, I believe, are all valid.\n\n**1) the traditional way**\n\nIFS books describe Self as the “absence of parts”. In other words, by asking parts to step aside, people automatically experience the state that we call Self. In this state, people experience calm, clarity of thought, courage and compassion.\n\n**2) the spiritual way**\n\nThe traditional definition of Self seems very close to the idea of “Higher Self” or of “Spirit” or “Inner wisdom” that is in common in various spiritual traditions. It is a state of consciousness characterised by love, calm and compassion.\n\n**3) The “undamaged” part of yourself**\n\nThis is one of my favourite because it instantly gives me hope and reminds me that, whatever difficulty or problem one person might be facing, there is more to life. We are more than our anxieties and mental health problems, and there is a part of us that is not damaged and that has not been touched by trauma. This undamaged Self not only exists, but it always available to create healing.\n\n## Remember that “*We are more than our parts*”\n\nSo… who are we? I like to think that we are the result of the interaction of Self and of our parts. When we are too separate from Self and our parts do not have much Self-energy available, we over-identify with parts and with the pain, traumas and distorted views of reality that these parts might carry.\n\nThe most important (and at times difficult) task to improve our mental health is to remember that there is always more to us and our lives than the pains and problems we are experiencing in that moment of life.\n\nIt is, unfortunately, difficult to experience Self without the assistance of an IFS trained therapist. Even if we can all experience Self, it takes time and guidance to access it consciously.\n\nAt times, if the internal system of a person is particularly burdened and heavily protected, it can take more than one IFS Sessions to experience a sense of Self, from which healing can happen.\n\n**One final note…**\n\nI hope this article has given you some insight into the IFS method. I personally write these articles and they are freely available on my website. I kindly ask that you reference them by quoting this webpage if you use them for personal, professional or educational purposes.\n\nIf there are topics you would like to know more about, please let me know.\n\nSelected Articles to inspire you\n\n![The first chakra - our connection to Mother Earth and feminine energy]()\n![Getting out of the Drama Triangle]()\n![The energetic nature of trauma and how it affects our life]()\n![Covid-19 anxiety for people living away from their family]()\n![Corona virus (Covid-19) Anxiety]()\n\n**Alessio Rizzo (He | They) MA, MSc**\n\nIFS Trainer, Approved Clinical Consultant and Level 3 Certified Psychotherapist - specialised in LGBTQIA+ and neurodiversity.\n\nAlessio keeps his blog free and without adverts to help and support everyone. His podcast “Queering IFS” can be accessed [here](/queering-ifs-podcast).\n\nPlease consider supporting this website by visiting the [free downloads page](/free-ifs-digital-downloads), or purchasing a copy of the [Parts’ Journal](/ifs-parts-journal), or, for trained IFS professional, the [IFS for ADHD online course](https://ifs.onlinecoursehost.com/courses/ifs-for-adhd-5th-apr-24).\n\n[Terms and Conditions & Privacy Policy](/website-terms-and-conditions-and-privacy-policy)\n\n![](https://images.squarespace-cdn.com/content/v1/5cd007b4348cd92afabde0b1/1da7d92d-ac48-41e8-b652-92ea0f738e19/IFS+Trainer+Badge.png)\n![](https://images.squarespace-cdn.com/content/v1/5cd007b4348cd92afabde0b1/e9736f8a-7648-4e55-be5e-f97f633ba920/Approved+Clinical+Consultant+Badge.png)\n![](https://images.squarespace-cdn.com/content/v1/5cd007b4348cd92afabde0b1/f17b83ce-a241-4517-b7e8-16bd6971005d/IFS+Certified+Therapist+Badge.png)\n\n## Subscribe to receive my latest articles\n\nSign up with your email address to receive news and updates.\n\nWe respect your privacy.\n\nThank you!\n\nTitle: Internal Family Systems Therapy: 8 Worksheets and Exercises\nURL: https://positivepsychology.com/internal-family-systems-therapy/\nContent: IFS Therapy finds ways to help our ego relax, allowing those parts of our personality we have buried (exiles) to ascend, freeing memories, emotions, and beliefs associated with them (burdens) that were previously locked away (Schwartz, 2021). IFS Therapy helps clients form a deeply satisfying relationship with themselves and others, unburdening their trauma and accessing their self-energy (Herbine-Blank, n.d.). Therapy using the IFS model addresses and communicates with these parts, attempting to help the client find balance and harmony within their mind and elevate their self to the system’s leadership. IFS Therapy uses self-compassion to encourage buried parts of our personality to ascend, freeing memories, emotions, and previously locked-away beliefs.\nRaw Content: 20 Oct 2025\n\n [23](#comment-list)\n\n# Internal Family Systems Therapy: 8 Worksheets and Exercises\n\n**25 Feb 2022** by  **[Jeremy Sutton, Ph.D.](https://positivepsychology.com/team/jeremy-sutton/)**\n\nScientifically reviewed by  **[Jo Nash, Ph.D.](https://positivepsychology.com/team/jo-nash/)**\n\n### Key Insights\n\n11 minute read\n\n* Internal Family Systems (IFS) therapy explores the idea that the mind is composed of multiple sub-personalities, or “parts,” each with its own roles.\n* IFS helps individuals understand & integrate these parts, fostering healing & internal harmony.\n* Emphasizing self-compassion & internal leadership, IFS can lead to improved mental health & personal growth.\n\nWhat if the idea that we each have a single personality is entirely wrong?\n\nInstead of the *one-mind view*, maybe you, I, and everyone else have multiple personalities.\n\nRichard Schwartz (2021), the creator of the Internal Family Systems (IFS) Therapy model, suggests we have all been born with *many* sub-minds interacting with one another.\n\nHaven’t we all heard conflicting inner voices saying “Go for it” and “Don’t you dare” (Sweezy & Ziskind, 2013)?\n\nThis article explores how the IFS model helps treat individuals and couples by directing these inner players, while introducing several tools and techniques to help the process.\n\nBefore you continue, we thought you might like to [download our five positive psychology tools for free](https://pro.positivepsychology.com/wp-content/uploads/giveawaytools.pdf). These detailed, science-based exercises will help you increase the compassion and kindness you show yourself and will also give you the tools to help your clients, students, or employees show more compassion to themselves.\n\n## This Article Contains\n\n* [What Is Internal Family Systems Therapy?](#what-is-internal-family-systems-therapy)\n* [6 Internal Family Systems Therapy Worksheets](#6-internal-family-systems-therapy-worksheets)\n* [Top 2 IFS Exercises for Your Sessions](#top-2-ifs-exercises-for-your-sessions)\n* [Resources From PositivePsychology.com](#resources-from-positivepsychology-com)\n* [A Take-Home Message](#a-take-home-message)\n* [Frequently Asked Questions](#section_faq)\n* [References](#references)\n\n## What Is Internal Family Systems Therapy?\n\nThe idea that “the mind is not a singular entity or self, but is multiple, composed of parts” is at the core of Richard Schwartz’s internal family systems (IFS) model (Sweezy & Ziskind, 2013, p. xviii).\n\nAccording to Schwartz (2021, p. 6), thinking involves *parts* “talking to each other and to you constantly about things you have to do or debating the best course of action, and so on.”\n\nEach *part* has its own beliefs, feelings, and characteristics and a distinct role in the overall system. They are clustered into the following three groups (Sweezy & Ziskind, 2013; IFS Institute, n.d.):\n\n* *Managers*  \n   Managers are a protective group of parts that attempt to keep us organized and safe, running our day-to-day lives. Over time, they may push for [perfectionism](https://positivepsychology.com/how-to-overcome-perfectionism/) and even inflict harm in their pursuit of safety.\n* *Exiles*  \n   Exiles are the injured parts of us and have typically experienced trauma. Exiled by the managers, they can become increasingly extreme, ultimately overriding the managers to become who we are.\n* *Firefighters*  \n   Firefighters are another form of protection that “put out the emotional fire at any cost,” often starting backfires. They do so in several ways, including unhealthy or unhelpful behavior, such as alcohol and drug abuse and eating disorders (Sweezy & Ziskind, 2013, p. xviii).\n\nOther theories relying on the single or *mono-mind* model may lead us to fear or dislike ourselves, believing “we only have one mind (full of primitive or sinful aspects) that we can’t control” (Schwartz, 2021, p. 12). Therapies based on this model often require clients to “correct irrational beliefs or meditate them away” (Schwartz, 2021, p. 12).\n\nOn the other hand, Schwartz’s IFS model argues that the ego comprises multiple *parts* trying to keep us safe. IFS Therapy finds ways to help our ego relax, allowing those parts of our personality we have buried (exiles) to ascend, freeing memories, emotions, and beliefs associated with them (burdens) that were previously locked away (Schwartz, 2021).\n\nWith this in mind, Schwartz’s (2021, p. 32) IFS Therapy has four goals:\n\n1. To liberate parts from the roles they have been forced into, freeing them to be who they were designed to be.\n2. To restore faith in the self and in [self-leadership](https://positivepsychology.com/self-leadership/).\n3. To re-harmonize the inner system.\n4. To encourage the client to become increasingly self led in their interactions with the world.\n\nWhile IFS Therapy is a powerful approach for helping *individuals*, it can be equally successful with *couples*. According to Herbine-Blank (n.d.), “once the individuals in a couple have more access to Self, transformation is natural,” and they can find the space and capacity to choose a response rather than simply react to it, even if the other cannot at the time.\n\nEach partner is encouraged to bring compassion to their wounded inner parts and heal their past, gaining control over their present (Herbine-Blank, n.d.).\n\n## 6 Internal Family Systems Therapy Worksheets\n\nIFS Therapy helps clients form a deeply satisfying relationship with themselves and others, unburdening their trauma and accessing their self-energy (Herbine-Blank, n.d.).\n\nWhile there are many aspects to IFS as a theory and treatment model, the primary healing relationship “is between the client’s Self and her young, injured parts” (Sweezy & Ziskind, 2013, p. 1).\n\nThe notion of *parts* is crucial to the IFS model. Therapy using the IFS model addresses and communicates with these parts, attempting to help the client find balance and harmony within their mind and elevate their self to the system’s leadership. To those unfamiliar with IFS Therapy, the language and conversation style may seem unusual, involving talking directly to the different parts of the self (IFS Institute, n.d.).\n\nThe following worksheets offer a range of tools for facilitating various aspects of this powerful and complex treatment to engage with and better understand the many parts of the self (modified from Anderson, Sweezy, & Schwartz, 2017; Sweezy & Ziskind, 2013; Schwartz, 2021):\n\n### All Parts Are Welcome\n\nThe [All Parts Are Welcome](https://positive.b-cdn.net/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/All-Parts-Are-Welcome.pdf) exercise was created by Schwartz and his team to help the client welcome all parts of their self, using their attention and a few simple questions (Anderson et al., 2017).\n\n### The Six Fs\n\nA key aspect of IFS Therapy is to *“find, focus* on and *flesh* out” the client’s protective parts and help them “unblend and notice the client’s Self” (Anderson et al., 2017, p. 93). Next, the client can recognize their *feelings* toward and *befriend* the target part, explore its *fears*, and invite it to do something new.\n\nExperienced practitioners can use the [Six Fs Internal Family Systems](https://positive.b-cdn.net/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/6Fs-Internal-Family-System.pdf) worksheet to successfully differentiate the protective parts from the self and form vital alliances.\n\n## World’s Largest Positive Psychology Resource\n\n*The Positive Psychology Toolkit©* is a groundbreaking practitioner resource containing over 500 science-based exercises, activities, interventions, questionnaires, and assessments created by experts using the latest positive psychology research.\n\nUpdated monthly. 100% science-based.\n\n*“The best positive psychology resource out there!”*   \n — Emiliya Zhivotovskaya, Flourishing Center CEO\n\n[Get Instant Access](https://pro.positivepsychology.com/sale-page/positive-psychology-toolkit/)\n\n### Understanding Our Relationship With a *Part*\n\nAfter identifying a part of the self, clients can explore it in greater detail to better understand whether it is doing its job.\n\nUse the [Understanding Our Relationship With a *Part*](https://positive.b-cdn.net/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/Understanding-Our-Relationship-With-a-Part.pdf) worksheet to identify the role and intent of the part under scrutiny by asking a series of questions:\n\n1. What is its role, and how does it help you manage your life?\n2. What is its relationship with other people?\n3. What positive intent does it have for you?\n4. How does it try to protect you?\n5. What is it trying to protect you from?\n6. Is it happy with its job? Or would it prefer something else?\n\nAs clients focus and make notes during the exercise, they may find that their relationship toward the part changes.\n\n### Identifying Parts of Yourself Through Drawing\n\nIt is not easy to recognize all the parts of the self. Drawing or doodling can provide a more intuitive, less concrete way to capture, describe, and show the connections between each part.\n\nIn the [Identifying Parts of Yourself Through Drawing](https://positive.b-cdn.net/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/Identifying-Parts-of-Yourself-Through-Drawing.pdf) worksheet, clients can create a picture to capture the various parts of the self and how they combine.\n\nDrawing the picture and working through this exercise can help them form a clearer understanding of the parts and their relationship to the self.\n\n### Identifying Managers and Firefighters\n\nWhile *managers* help us plan and shape our lives and avoid discomfort and pain, *firefighters* rush in and try to fix the problem. We may recognize both in how we cope. For example, when life is calm, we may plan; when we’re hectic or stressed, we may eat or drink too much.\n\nThe [Identifying Managers and Firefighters Using IFS Therapy](https://positive.b-cdn.net/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/Identifying-Managers-and-Firefighters-using-IFS-Therapy.pdf) worksheet helps identify manager and firefighter parts using the senses and asking a series of questions, including:\n\n* What bodily sensations accompany the part?\n* How do you feel regarding each part?\n* How does each part feel about you?\n\nIt should be clear from the answers whether the part is a manager or a firefighter.\n\n### What the Self Is and Isn’t in IFS Therapy\n\nHaving used IFS Therapy with many clients to explore their self, Schwartz (2021) identified eight Cs engaged in self-energy and self-leadership shared by almost everyone.\n\nThe [What the Self Is and Isn’t in IFS Therapy](https://positive.b-cdn.net/wp-content/uploads/2021/12/What-the-Self-Is-and-Isnt-in-IFS-Therapy.pdf) worksheet explores the eight Cs, encourages the client to notice the quality in themselves, and asks what each means to them:\n\n* *Curiosity*\n* *Calm*\n* *Confidence*\n* *Compassion*\n* *Creativity*\n* *Clarity*\n* *Courage*\n* *Connectedness*\n\nConsidering each one will make you feel more connected to humanity. And “when people sense how connected they are to humanity, they feel more curious about others and have more courage to help them” (Schwartz, 2021, p. 93).\n\nDr. Richard Schwartz explains Internal Family Systems (IFS)\n\n## Top 2 IFS Exercises for Your Sessions\n\nRichard Schwartz’s (2021) latest book, *No Bad Parts*, is an accessible read for those interested in his IFS approach to therapy, clarifying the nature of parts and the techniques to uncover them. According to Schwartz (2021. p. 17), “each part is like a person with a true purpose” that can be uncovered.\n\nThe following exercises are two of the most powerful techniques in this fascinating and powerful model (simplified from Schwartz, 2021):\n\n### The path of self\n\nThis exercise uses visualization to explore self and self-energy, using the metaphor of a journey.\n\nAsk the client to perform the following steps:\n\n* Find a comfortable position and begin by taking slow, deep breaths.\n* Imagine yourself meeting your parts at the beginning of a path.\n* Ask the parts to wait there as you head off on a journey.\n* Notice how they react. Are they afraid? Some days, they may not want you to go. And that’s okay. You can wait for another day to continue.\n* If it’s okay to proceed, head out on your imagined journey.\n* As you progress, if you find you are still *thinking* or *watching yourself*, then some parts are likely to have remained with you. See if they are willing to stay behind. Repeat as many times as required.\n* As you remove parts*,* feel yourself becoming lighter, moving toward pure awareness without thought.\n* You should begin to experience, among other things, clarity, a sense of wellbeing, and confidence.\n* Invite the energy you are feeling into your body.\n* Pause and experience what it is like to have so much *self* in your body.\n* When you’re ready, take some deep breaths and return your focus to the room.\n\nRepeat this exercise regularly, trying to remember how it feels throughout your day.\n\n## Download 5 Free Positive Psychology Tools\n\nStart thriving today with 5 free tools grounded in the science of positive psychology.\n\nDownload Tools\n\n### Fire drill\n\nThe following reflective exercise is a valuable way to revisit parts in life and learn self-leadership through practice and experience.\n\nAsk the client to perform the following steps:\n\n* Picture someone in your life (past or present) who triggers anger or sadness in you.\n* Imagine them in a room from which they cannot leave.\n* Watch the person from outside the room through a one-way mirror. You can see them, but they cannot see you.\n* Now have them do or say the things that upset you.\n* Notice what happens to your body and mind as your *protector* part kicks in (your muscles, heart rate, breathing, thoughts, and emotions).\n* Now look at the person through the eyes of your protector. Reassure your protector that you will not be entering that room.\n* Encourage the protector to recognize that you are not at risk, so it can stand down.\n* See if your protector is prepared to separate its energy from you. Encourage it to take the energy away.\n* Now recheck yourself. What changes do you notice in your muscles, heart rate, breathing, thoughts, and emotions?\n* How does the person in the room look now?\n* How would it feel if you were to go into the room and be with that person, *self* led rather than accompanied by the protector?\n* Ask the protector if it can begin to trust you. If not, why not?\n* When you’re ready, thank the protector for the support it has given you and the new trust it has in you.\n* Take some deep breaths and return your focus to the room.\n\n“If your protector did step to the side, you probably noticed a big shift” (Schwartz, 2021, p. 130). Repeating the exercise can help clients learn about the parts that protect and how vulnerable they have previously been.\n\n## 17 Exercises To Foster Self-Acceptance and Compassion\n\nHelp your clients develop a kinder, more accepting relationship with themselves using these **17 Self-Compassion Exercises [PDF]** that promote self-care and self-compassion.\n\n*Created by Experts. 100% Science-based.*\n\n[Download Now](https://pro.positivepsychology.com/product/17-self-compassion-exercises/)\n\n## Resources From PositivePsychology.com\n\nSelf-compassion is a vital aspect of IFS Therapy. We have various resources that can help clients look at themselves with more kindness, including:\n\n* *I Will Survive*  \n   Reflect on a past event and [identify the strengths](https://positive.b-cdn.net/wp-content/uploads/I-Will-Survive.pdf) that helped you cope.\n* *Easing Empathy Distress*  \n   Feeling another’s pain or sadness can lead to empathy distress. [This tool](https://positive.b-cdn.net/wp-content/uploads/2020/09/Easing-“Empathy-Distress”-with-Compassion-Worksheet.pdf) helps turn empathy into compassion.\n\nWhile this article may offer you an interesting read:\n\n* [What Are Family Therapy & Family Counseling?](https://positivepsychology.com/family-therapy/)\n\nMore extensive versions of the following tools are available with a subscription to the [Positive Psychology Toolkit©](https://pro.positivepsychology.com/product/positive-psychology-toolkit-yearly-new/), but they are described briefly below:\n\n* *From Inner Critic to Inner Coach Meditation*\n\nThis tool aims to help clients differentiate between their threat defense system (i.e., their inner critic) and their caregiving system (i.e., their inner coach), learning to let go of the former.\n\nAsk the client to:\n\n* + Practice mindful meditation, bringing to mind a recent time when they were critical or judgmental of themselves.\n  + Notice their inner critic and how it makes them feel.\n  + Now imagine replacing the inner critic with an inner coach.\n  + What would it say to them, and how would they feel?\n* *Embracing Your Humanness*\n\nWe use this tool to help people cultivate self-compassion by developing an appreciation for common humanity in a group setting.\n\nGuide the members of the group through the following steps:\n\n* + Step one – Write down one self-criticism on a piece of paper.\n  + Step two – Collect all the folded notes.\n  + Step three – Choose and share a note at random.\n  + Step four – Discuss self-criticism within the group and share common experiences.\n\nThe aim is to recognize that each person in the group is not alone in having self-critical thoughts.\n\nIf you want to guide others in cultivating self-compassion and emotional wellbeing, our [Self-Compassion X©](https://pro.positivepsychology.com/product/self-compassion-x/) training program is the right tool for you. Designed for practitioners to use anywhere, it is filled with ready-to-use practices.\n\nLastly, if you’re looking for more science-based ways to help others develop self-compassion, check out [this collection of 17 validated self-compassion tools](https://pro.positivepsychology.com/product/17-self-compassion-exercises/) for practitioners. Use them to help others create a kinder and more nurturing relationship with the self.\n\n## A Take-Home Message\n\nThe internal family systems model proposes that the mind is not a singular entity or self but is composed of multiple parts that try to keep us safe (Sweezy & Ziskind, 2013).\n\nIFS Therapy uses self-compassion to encourage buried parts of our personality to ascend, freeing memories, emotions, and previously locked-away beliefs.\n\nNot only that, it enables clients to unburden trauma, access self-energy, and form deeply satisfying relationships with themselves and others.\n\nWhile IFS is a complex, sometimes unlikely, theory, it does provide a valuable model for therapy, healing the relationship between the client’s self and their “young, injured parts” (Sweezy & Ziskind, 2013, p. 1).\n\nThe treatment offers hope to clients wishing to find balance and harmony within their mind and facilitate the self to regain control.\n\nWhy not try out some of the worksheets and exercises with your clients to help the self, rather than the therapist, become the primary, caring attachment figure necessary to heal the client’s injured parts?\n\nWe hope you enjoyed reading this article. Don’t forget to [download our five positive psychology tools for free](https://pro.positivepsychology.com/wp-content/uploads/giveawaytools.pdf).\n\n## Frequently Asked Questions\n\nWhat is Internal Family Systems (IFS) therapy?\n\nIFS therapy is a psychotherapy approach that views the mind as a system of distinct subpersonalities or “parts,” each with its own perspective and role. Developed by Dr. Richard Schwartz, it aims to achieve internal harmony by understanding and integrating these parts.\n\nWhat are the main types of parts in IFS therapy?\n\nIn IFS, parts are categorized into Managers, Exiles, and Firefighters. Managers protect by controlling daily life, Exiles carry past traumas, and Firefighters react impulsively to distract from pain.\n\nWhat are the benefits of IFS therapy?\n\nIFS therapy can reduce symptoms of anxiety and depression, enhance self-compassion, and improve emotional wellbeing by promoting internal harmony and self-leadership.\n\nReferences\n\n* Anderson, F., Sweezy, M., & Schwartz, R. (2017). *Internal family systems skills training manual: Trauma-informed treatment for anxiety, depression, PTSD & substance abuse*. PESI.\n* Herbine-Blank, T. (n.d.). *Couples & marriage counseling with internal family systems therapy*. IFS Institute. Retrieved November 18, 2021, from <https://ifs-institute.com/resources/articles/couples-marriage-counseling-internal-family-systems-therapy>\n* IFS Institute. (n.d.). *The internal family systems model outline*. Retrieved November 18, 2021, from <https://ifs-institute.com/resources/articles/internal-family-systems-model-outline>\n* Schwartz, R. C. (2021). *No bad parts: Healing trauma and restoring wholeness with the internal family systems model*. Sounds True.\n* Sweezy, M., & Ziskind, E. L. (2013). *Internal family systems therapy: New dimensions*. Routledge.\n\nAbout the author\n\n[Jeremy Sutton, Ph.D.](https://positivepsychology.com/team/jeremy-sutton/), is an experienced psychologist, coach, consultant, and psychology lecturer. He works with individuals and groups to promote resilience, mental toughness, strength-based coaching, emotional intelligence, wellbeing, and flourishing. I believe that would be impossible, and trying so would go against how we are composed. As the Richard Schwartz 2021 book says in the title “No Bad Parts”. The goal is to let go of the burdens of the exiles so that the ego/protector parts can fall back and into their natural roles where they are active or activated when needed. Opposed to being in an extreme role 24/7. The latter being taxing on our body. A couple of years ago I was working with a client who was letting go of a lot of grief. Then one day the client was exposed to manipulation by a partner where the client immediately reacted firmly without inner conflict. The client surprised asked her-/himself “where the heck did that come from?”. I think this is a good example of a manager-protector previously being occupied by protecting an exile to becoming free to stand up for the client. Previously this would not be possible, because leaving the position of protecting the exile to stand up for the client would have left the exiled part exposed. The exiled part now unburdened made the manager part free to do what it is supposed to do. Which is natural preservation of the person when needed.\n\n       When children, and grownups, are doing well and at peace they are anchored in their whole body and self with the ability to just observe with no thoughts at all, but all parts are present. At this moment here is no need for achievement, management, or protection, so no part is activated.\n\n       [Reply](#comment-34543)\n\n       - Charlotte  on October 2, 2023 at 13:59\n\n         Yes this exactly. Therapy: The 8 C’s and 5 P’s A central tenet of Internal Family Systems (IFS) therapy, created by Richard Schwartz, is the concept of the Self. **The 8 C’s of the Self in IFS Therapy:** **The 5 P’s in Ifs Therapy EMDR & IFS-Informed therapist helping high-achieving women heal trauma. ## Exploring “Inside Out 2” Through the Lens of IFS and EMDR Therapy\nRaw Content: [Sitemap](/sitemap/sitemap.xml)\n\n[Open in app](https://rsci.app.link/?%24canonical_url=https%3A%2F%2Fmedium.com%2Fp%2F90c4a76476f1&%7Efeature=LoOpenInAppButton&%7Echannel=ShowPostUnderUser&%7Estage=mobileNavBar&source=post_page---top_nav_layout_nav-----------------------------------------)\n\n[Sign in](/m/signin?operation=login&redirect=https%3A%2F%2Fmedium.com%2F%40morgan_85152%2Funderstanding-self-in-ifs-therapy-the-8-cs-and-5-p-s-90c4a76476f1&source=post_page---top_nav_layout_nav-----------------------global_nav------------------)\n\n[Sign in](/m/signin?operation=login&redirect=https%3A%2F%2Fmedium.com%2F%40morgan_85152%2Funderstanding-self-in-ifs-therapy-the-8-cs-and-5-p-s-90c4a76476f1&source=post_page---top_nav_layout_nav-----------------------global_nav------------------)\n\nWriting is for everyone.[Register for Medium Day](https://events.zoom.us/ev/Av7REBItl8l_9abuYg_Iyhrgx4cwt8FEGYhzPou4dCMBDIhOV8ZQ~AmiyQniI6sZwr3sSvUHXWMpdX5wpciIv0a3EWsjOm0kEgiush-6TTsavY_EhDomBRAK8a2foXpncjXcEQADVKgkbMA?source=---medium_day_banner-----------------------------------------)\n\n# Understanding Self in IFS Therapy: The 8 C’s and 5 P’s\n\n[Morgan Levine, LCSW](/@morgan_85152?source=post_page---byline--90c4a76476f1---------------------------------------)\n\n3 min readJun 7, 2024\n\nA central tenet of Internal Family Systems (IFS) therapy, created by Richard Schwartz, is the concept of the Self. At the core of IFS lies the belief that each individual possesses a wise center, often referred to as the Self.\n\nThe Self in IFS is defined by the “8 C’s” and “5 P’s”. The 8 C’s are Curiosity, Compassion, Calm, Clarity, Courage, Confidence, Creativity, and Connectedness. The 5 P’s are Presence, Perspective, Patience, Persistence, and Playfulness. Let’s take a look at what these mean and how they can help us understand ourselves better.\n\n**The 8 C’s of the Self in IFS Therapy:**\n\n**Curiosity:** This means being open-minded and wanting to understand things instead of judging them.\n\n**Compassion:** It’s all about being kind and understanding, especially to our own feelings of hurt or sadness.\n\n**Calm:** This is about feeling centered and peaceful inside, even when things are tough.\n\n**Clarity:** It’s like having a clear picture of what we want and who we are, which helps us know where we’re going.\n\n**Courage:** This means being brave enough to face our fears and explore our feelings.\n\n**Confidence:** It’s believing in ourselves and knowing we’re worth it, even when we feel unsure.\n\n**Creativity:** The Self is full of new ideas and ways to express ourselves.\n\n## Get Morgan Levine, LCSW’s stories in your inbox\n\nJoin Medium for free to get updates from this writer.\n\n**Connectedness:** Feeling connected to ourselves, others, and the world around us.\n\n**The 5 P’s in IFS Therapy:**\n\n**Presence:** Being present and aware of what’s happening inside us without getting overwhelmed.\n\n**Perspective:** Seeing things from a bigger view, beyond just how we feel right now.\n\n**Patience:** Being okay with taking things slow and not rushing our healing journey.\n\n**Persistence:** Keep going, even when things get tough, because we know it’s worth it.\n\n**Playfulness:** Approaching life with a light-hearted attitude and finding joy in exploring ourselves.\n\nIn IFS therapy, these qualities help us understand and heal ourselves. By practicing the 8 C’s and 5 P’s, we can discover who we really are and feel better inside.To start using these qualities in your own life, try noticing moments when you feel curious, compassionate, present, courageous, etc. Then, try bringing that same energy to your thoughts and feelings. It’s a personal journey, and it’s okay if it takes time.\n\nIf you want help along the way, talking to an IFS-informed therapist can be really helpful. Exploring these qualities can lead us to a deeper understanding of ourselves and a more authentic way of living.\n\n**Authorship:** This blog was written by [Morgan Levine, LCSW](https://ifsemdrtherapy.com/morgan-levine). Morgan specializes in working with adults (18+) through the lens of neurodiversity, grief, and complex trauma. Her clients are people from across the lifespan and lived experiences who experience anxiety, depression, PTSD, ADHD, obsessions and compulsions, relationship issues, CPTSD, use of substances or people, attachment wounds, and specific trauma events.\n\n**Disclaimer:** The information in this blog is provided for educational and informational purposes only. more recommendations](/?source=post_page---read_next_recirc--90c4a76476f1---------------------------------------)\n\n[Text to speech](https://speechify.com/medium?source=post_page-----90c4a76476f1---------------------------------------)\n\nTitle: My IFS Cheat Sheet - Medium\nURL: https://medium.com/@braintots/my-ifs-cheat-sheet-7dada3fb0e18\nContent: The protector has likely been guarding an exile. It’s time to ask the protector and the exile if you can work with the exile. * Ask the Protector to show you the Exile. * Ask the Protector for permission to get to know the exile. * Ask the Protector what it is afraid would happen if you talk to the Exile. Ask the Exile to step back from control so that you can get to know it. * Ask the Exile to contain its feelings, so you can be there for it. * Ask the Exile how it has made you feel. * Ask the Exile how it would like to release its burdens.\nRaw Content: [Sitemap](/sitemap/sitemap.xml)\n\n[Open in app](https://rsci.app.link/?%24canonical_url=https%3A%2F%2Fmedium.com%2Fp%2F7dada3fb0e18&%7Efeature=LoOpenInAppButton&%7Echannel=ShowPostUnderUser&%7Estage=mobileNavBar&source=post_page---top_nav_layout_nav-----------------------------------------)\n\n[Sign in](/m/signin?operation=login&redirect=https%3A%2F%2Fmedium.com%2F%40braintots%2Fmy-ifs-cheat-sheet-7dada3fb0e18&source=post_page---top_nav_layout_nav-----------------------global_nav------------------)\n\n[Search](/search?source=post_page---top_nav_layout_nav-----------------------------------------)\n\n[Sign in](/m/signin?operation=login&redirect=https%3A%2F%2Fmedium.com%2F%40braintots%2Fmy-ifs-cheat-sheet-7dada3fb0e18&source=post_page---top_nav_layout_nav-----------------------global_nav------------------)\n\n# My IFS Cheat Sheet\n\n[braintots](/@braintots?source=post_page---byline--7dada3fb0e18---------------------------------------)\n\n6 min read\n\n·\n\nFeb 11, 2024\n\n--\n\nThis is the list of steps, and sample questions, I go through in my Internal Family Systems self-practice. It is based on the book [Self Therapy](https://www.amazon.com/Self-Therapy-Step-Step-Cutting-Edge-Psychotherapy/dp/0578395940?crid=1I2ZSXRCAK0R1&dib=eyJ2IjoiMSJ9.s0gtJUIFwTly_6oieGdgx-3QEbxojAZtmRIS4-umr8BSawm8KSSx4_d0RaS_2tWY7DPGrwJWrKFJn2bWPdAJstPfsWG8Gn9QKQF-s1FzrK8gQrUGzPnIcAETOu2QNLmK_LQS6UjgPumgd3w_5-DQ5djCk_0z5TpSncIdZ1NJlL5sfApXN3rwsdjE4cq1hVJSGyLdOYBqR8ZHkVAEkiPlRIM0oaxkjtSwatD3B0abLsU.FyTzIdM9fJQFCxixMkX_tg3BCVJC2xdDwAXFSI-OB7s&dib_tag=se&keywords=self+therapy+jay+earley&qid=1722019969&sprefix=Self+Therapy%2Caps%2C232&sr=8-1&linkCode=ll1&tag=2potatoes-20&linkId=9c75636d2a9b2f696e976bb371d47eae&language=en_US&ref_=as_li_ss_tl) by Jay Early (affiliate link.)\n\nI keep these steps and sample questions on a piece of paper next to me, and it helps keep me from getting “lost” while doing the work.\n\n(A list of the other articles I’ve written about my IFS journey is available in my article [My Internal Family Systems Experience](/@braintots/my-internal-family-systems-experience-20aac247e626).)\n\n## 1. Get to know a Protector\n\n## P1. Accessing a Protector\n\nFind a part that you’d like to work with. You might:\n\n* Ask and see if there are any parts that would like to do the work now.\n* Access a trailhead. If there aren’t any parts volunteering to do the work, think of a recent situation where a part was activated, and recall the situation.\n\n## P2. Unblend from the Target Protector\n\nSeparate yourself from the part, exploring it as a separate entity within you. You might:\n\n* Ask the part to separate from you, so you can get to know it,\n* Move back, internally, to separate yourself from the part,\n* See an image of the part at a distance.\n\n## P3. Unblend from any Concerned Parts\n\nSeparate yourself from any parts that are concerned with the target part. This might be a part that is arguing with the target part, or blocking it from being seen. Start by:\n\n* Checking to see how you feel about the target part. If it is anything other than “compassionate and curious” (“Self”), try to separate from the concerned part.\n* Ask the concerned part if it would be willing to step aside for a bit.\n* Ask the concerned part what it is afraid of if it were to step aside.\n* If it won’t step aside, after a bit of explanation, ask it if it is willing to become the target part.\n\nRepeat the steps above with all concerned parts, until you are feeling compassionate and curious about the target part.\n\n## P4. Discover the Protector’s Role\n\nGet to know the protector. Ask it questions like:\n\n* What do you feel? (I like to touch the areas of my body where I am feeling sensations/tension.)\n* What are you concerned about?\n* What is your role? What do you do to perform this role?\n* What do you hope to accomplish by playing this role?\n* What are you afraid would happen if you didn’t perform this role?\n\n## P5. Build Trust with the Protector\n\nExpress your curiosity and compassion to the protector. If they are true, you might consider the following:\n\n* “I understand why you are performing this role / doing what you do.”\n* “I appreciate your efforts on my behalf.”\n* “I know you’ve been working very hard.”\n\nIf you are not feeling these things, explore step **P3** again, and proceed from there.\n\n## 2. Obtain Permission to work with the Exile\n\nThe protector has likely been guarding an exile. It’s time to ask the protector and the exile if you can work with the exile.\n\n* Ask the Protector to show you the Exile.\n* Ask the Protector for permission to get to know the exile.\n\nIf the Protector is unwilling:\n\n* Ask the Protector what it is afraid would happen if you talk to the Exile.\n* Reassure it that you can stay in Self and not be overwhelmed by the pain.\n* Reassure it that you are trying to heal the pain.\n* Reassure it that the Protector can take over again if it becomes uncomfortable. (I invite my Protectors to stand by and watch the whole process.)\n* Reassure the Protector that it can take on whatever role it wants within your system when the pain is healed.\n\nSometimes the answer will be “no”, and that’s okay. Let your Protector and Exile know that you understand they are not ready. “Self” will be here when they are ready. Ask for permission to confer with them another time and, when you do, start from the beginning again.\n\n## 3. Get to know the exile\n\n## E1. Access the Exile\n\nSense the Exile in your body, or get an image of the Exile.\n\n## E2. Unblend from the Exile\n\nAsk the Exile to step back from control so that you can get to know it. You might:\n\n* Ask the Exile to contain its feelings, so you can be there for it.\n* Consciously separate form the exile and return to Self.\n* Get an image of the exile at a distance from you.\n\nIf the exile won’t contain its feelings:\n\n* Ask it what it is afraid would happen if it relinquished control for a while.\n* Explain that you really want to witness its feelings and story, but you need to separate from it in order to do that.\n\n## E3. Unblend from Concerned Parts\n\nCheck how you feel towards the Exile. If it is not compassionate and curious, follow the same steps as outlined in P3 until you are able to find your compassion for the Exile.\n\n## E4. Get to know the Exile\n\nAsk it:\n\n* What do you feel?\n* What makes you feel so scared/hurt/whatever feelings it is feeling?\n\n## E5. Develop a trusting relationship\n\n* Listen to the Exile and hear its story\n* Communicate compassion and caring.\n* Check to see that the Exile can sense you there.\n* Notice if the Exile is feeling your compassion.\n\n## 4. Access and Witness the Exile’s childhood origins\n\n* Ask the Exile to show you an image or memory of when it learned to feel the way it feels.\n* Ask the Exile how it has made you feel.\n* Verify that the Exile has shown you everything it wanted you to witness.\n* Verify that the Exile believes that you understand how bad it was.\n\n## 5. Reparent the Exile\n\nBring “Self” to the childhood situation and ask the Exile what it needs from you to heal it or change what happened. It might be something like:\n\n* “rewriting” the story, or\n* comforting the Exile, or\n* standing up for the Exile\n\nGive the Exile what it needs in order to feel loved and cared for.\n\n## 6. Retrieve the Exile (Optional)\n\nIf the Exile is interested, offer to take it someplace else — a place of safety in your present life, or your body, or an imaginary place.\n\n(Note that I often do this step before Step 5.)\n\n## 7. Unburden the Exile\n\n* Name the burdens (extreme feelings or beliefs) that the Exile is carrying.\n* Ask the Exile if it wants to give up or release the burdens, and if it’s ready to do so.\n* If not, ask the Exile what it is afraid would happen if it let go ov them? Address any fears that might come up.\n* Ask the Exile how it carries the burdens in or on its body.\n* Ask the Exile how it would like to release its burdens. Recommendations tend to be to the core elements: light, water, wind and fire.\n* When the burdens are gone, notice the positive qualities of the Exile.\n\n## 8. Integrate and Unburden the Protector\n\nRevisit the protector from Step 1.\n\n* Introduce it to the transformed Exile.\n* Ask the Protector if it believes that its role of protection is still necessary.\n* If not, repeat Step 7 with the Protector as the target of the unburdening.\n* Invite the Protector to choose a new role within your system.\n\nFinally, imagine yourself in the original trailhead and see if any parts become activated. Work your way through all these steps for any additional parts that become activated.\n\n## Notes from my practice\n\n* I feel free to stop at any time along the path. Sometimes an Exile or a Protector just isn’t in the mood to do this work, and that’s okay. I’ll invite it to reach out when it’s ready to do the work, ask it for permission to check in again later, and go about the rest of my day.\n* I have 3 locations that most of my parts are retrieved to: the “Control Room”, the “Garden”, and the “Gazebo.” In each of these locations they can be with other parts.\n* Lately, most of my unburdenings end with the Parts releasing their burdens into the ground, which creates flower patches in the garden. It’s pretty nice.\n* Towards the beginning, most of my unburdenings ended with Fire or Lightning. The Fire was released into the Furnace (a Part) which is in the Control Room, and a personification of the energy that anger brings. (Lightning seems to be correlated for me with the type energy that nervousness brings.)\n* It can easily take me an hour to get through Step 1. It almost always takes me more than an hour-and-a-half to get through all of the steps.\n* I perform the unblending steps — P2, P3, E2 and E3 — pretty informally. Usually just the check “do I feel compassion for the target Part?”\n* But I am vigilant during the other steps to see if I’ve blended with a part. If I find that there’s an argument going on in my head (or unkindness, or anything other than curiosity), I’ll revisit the P3 and E3 steps, and then continue on.\n\n[Internal Family System](/tag/internal-family-system?source=post_page-----7dada3fb0e18---------------------------------------)\n\n[Self Therapy](/tag/self-therapy?source=post_page-----7dada3fb0e18---------------------------------------)\n\n[Parts Work](/tag/parts-work?source=post_page-----7dada3fb0e18---------------------------------------)\n\n[## Written by braintots](/@braintots?source=post_page---post_author_info--7dada3fb0e18---------------------------------------)\n\n[9 followers](/@braintots/followers?source=post_page---post_author_info--7dada3fb0e18---------------------------------------)\n\n·[1 following](/@braintots/following?source=post_page---post_author_info--7dada3fb0e18---------------------------------------)\n\nSteps to remember for dealing with... stuff\n\n## No responses yet\n\n[Text to speech](https://speechify.com/medium?source=post_page-----7dada3fb0e18---------------------------------------)\n\nTitle: The Internal Family Systems Model Outline | IFS Institute\nURL: https://ifs-institute.com/resources/articles/internal-family-systems-model-outline\nContent: PARTS** * All parts want something positive for the individual and will use a variety of strategies to gain influence within the internal system. 2. When the individual is \"in Self\" or when the Self is in the lead while interacting with others (day-to-day experience), the Self is experienced along with the non-extreme aspects of the parts. * When working with families, check for the family's awareness of parts in self and others. 1. Important to assess protective parts and work with them first. + Self to parts 2. Selves working together to keep extreme parts of each family member from interfering 2. Set up enactments of sequences/relationship among parts of individual family members. * General frame of Selves working together to keep extreme parts of each family member from interfering\nRaw Content: [Skip to main navigation](#main-menu)\n\n* [Home](/)\n* [About Us](/about-us)\n  + [Richard C. [Learning Objectives](/trainings/level-1/learning-objectives \"Level 1 IFS Training Learning Objectives\")\n  + [Level 2](/trainings/level-2)\n    - [North American Trainings](/trainings/level-2/north-american-trainings \"Level 2 IFS Trainings in United States and Canada\")\n    - [International Trainings](/trainings/level-2/international-trainings)\n    - [Learning Objectives](/trainings/level-2/learning-objectives \"Level 2 IFS Intensive Training Learning Objectives\")\n  + [Level 3](/trainings/level-3)\n    - [North American Trainings](/trainings/level-3/north-american-trainings \"Level 3 IFS Trainings in US and Canada\")\n    - [International Trainings](/trainings/level-3/international-trainings)\n    - [Learning Objectives](/trainings/level-3/learning-objectives \"Level 3 Training Learning Objectives\")\n  + [IFS Foundations for Coaches](https://ifs-institute.com/trainings/foundations-for-coaches)\n  + [Program Assistants](/trainings/program-assistants)\n    - [Applications](/trainings/program-assistants/applications)\n    - [PA Development](/trainings/program-assistants/pa-development \"Program Assistant Development\")\n  + [Email Notifications](/trainings/email-notifications)\n  + [Scholarships](/trainings/scholarships \"Scholarships for North American IFS Trainings\")\n* [IFS Certification](/ifs-certification)\n  + [IFS Certification Policy](/ifs-certification/ifs-certification-policy)\n  + [IFS Certification Reviewers](/ifs-certification/ifs-certification-reviewers)\n  + [IFS Recertification](/recertification)\n  + [IFS Approved Clinical Consultants](/trainings/ifs-certification/approved-clinical-consultants)\n  + [IFS Digital Badges](/ifs-digital-badging)\n* [Online Learning](/online-learning)\n* [Conference](/conference)\n  + [Lifetime Achievement Award](/conference/lifetime-achievement \"IFS Lifetime Achievement Award\")\n* Resources\n  + [Help Center](https://info.ifs-institute.com/ifsi-help-center)\n  + [Articles](/resources/articles)\n  + [Videos](/resources/videos)\n  + [Podcasts and Teleconferences](/resources/podcasts-and-teleconferences)\n  + [Community](/resources/community)\n  + [IFS Foundation](/resources/ifs-foundation)\n  + [Research and Bibliography](/resources/research)\n  + [IFS Trainer Career Path](/trainer-career-path)\n* News & Events\n  + [News](/news-events/news)\n  + [Events](/news-events/events)\n* [Find a Professional](/practitioners \"Find and IFS Therapist\")\n* [Contact](/about-us/contacts)\n* [IFS store](/store)\n\n# The Internal Family Systems Model Outline\n\n**I. BASIC ASSUMPTIONS OF THE IFS MODEL**\n\n* It is the nature of the mind to be subdivided into an indeterminate number of subpersonalities or parts.\n* Everyone has a Self, and the Self can and should lead the individual's internal system.\n* The non-extreme intention of each part is something positive for the individual. There are no \"bad\" parts, and the goal of therapy is not to eliminate parts but instead to help them find their non-extreme roles.\n* As we develop, our parts develop and form a complex system of interactions among themselves; therefore, systems theory can be applied to the internal system. When the system is reorganized, parts can change rapidly.\n* Changes in the internal system will affect changes in the external system and vice versa. The implication of this assumption is that both the internal and external levels of system should be assessed.\n\n**II. OVERALL GOALS OF THERAPY**\n\n* To achieve balance and harmony within the internal system\n* To differentiate and elevate the Self so it can be an effective leader in the system\n* When the Self is in the lead, the parts will provide input to the Self but will respect the leadership and ultimate decision making of the Self.\n* All parts will exist and lend talents that reflect their non-extreme intentions.\n\n**III. PARTS**\n\n* Subpersonalities are aspects of our personality that interact internally in sequences and styles that are similar to the ways in which people interact.\n* Parts may be experienced in any number of ways -- thoughts, feelings, sensations, images, and more.\n* All parts want something positive for the individual and will use a variety of strategies to gain influence within the internal system.\n* Parts develop a complex system of interactions among themselves. Polarizations develop as parts try to gain influence within the system.\n* While experiences affect parts, parts are not created by the experiences. They are always in existence, either as potential or actuality.\n* Parts that become extreme are carrying \"burdens\" -- energies that are not inherent in the function of the part and don't belong to the nature of the part, such as extreme beliefs, emotions, or fantasies. Parts can be helped to \"unburden\" and return to their natural balance.\n* Parts that have lost trust in the leadership of the Self will \"blend\" with or take over the Self.\n\n**IV. SELF**\n\n* Different level of entity than the parts -- often in the center of the \"you\" that the parts are talking to or that likes or dislikes, listens to, or shuts out various parts\n* When differentiated, the Self is competent, secure, self-assured, relaxed, and able to listen and respond to feedback.\n* The Self can and should lead the internal system.\n* Various levels of experience of the Self:\n  1. When completely differentiated from all parts (Self alone), people describe a feeling of being \"centered.\"\n  2. When the individual is \"in Self\" or when the Self is in the lead while interacting with others (day-to-day experience), the Self is experienced along with the non-extreme aspects of the parts.\n* An empowering aspect of the model is that everyone has a Self.\n\n**V. GENERAL GROUPS OF PARTS**\n\n* EXILES\n  1. Young parts that have experienced trauma and often become isolated from the rest of the system in an effort to protect the individual from feeling the pain, terror, fear, and so on, of these parts\n  2. If exiled, can become increasingly extreme and desperate in an effort to be cared for and tell their story\n  3. Can leave the individual feeling fragile and vulnerable\n* MANAGERS\n  1. Parts that run the day-to-day life of the individual\n  2. Attempt to keep the individual in control of every situation and relationship in an effort to protect parts from feeling any hurt or rejection\n  3. Can do this in any number of ways or through a combination of parts -- striving, controlling, evaluating, caretaking, terrorizing, and so on.\n* FIREFIGHTERS\n  1. Group of parts that react when exiles are activated in an effort to control and extinguish their feelings\n  2. Can do this in any number of ways, including drug or alcohol use, self-mutilation (cutting), binge-eating, sex binges\n  3. Have the same goals as managers (to keep exiles away) but different strategies\n\n**VI. BEGINNING TO USE THE MODEL**\n\n* Assess client's parts and sequences around the problem.\n* Look for polarizations:\n  1. Within individuals\n  2. Among family members\n* Look for parallel dynamics: The way you relate to your own parts parallels the way you relate to those parts of others.\n* Introduce the language of the model.\n* Check for individual's awareness of parts -- ask how he or she experiences the part: thoughts, feelings, sensations, images, and so on.\n* When working with families, check for the family's awareness of parts in self and others.\n* Make a decision about how to begin using the model: language, direct access, imagery, and so on.\n* Come to agreement with client on initial goals of therapy in terms of the internal system -- create a \"contract.\"\n* Assess the fears of manager parts and value the roles of the managers; explain how the therapy can work without the feared outcomes of the managers happening.\n* Inventory dangerous firefighters; work with managers' fears about triggering firefighters.\n* Assess client's external context and constraints to doing this work.\n\n**VII. RELATIONSHIP BETWEEN INTERNAL AND EXTERNAL SYSTEMS**\n\n* The way you relate to your own parts parallels the way you relate to those parts of others.\n* Individual's internal system affects and is affected by the external system of which he or she is a part.\n* Internal and external systems often parallel each other.\n\n**VIII. WORKING WITH INDIVIDUALS**\n\n* Protective Parts\n  1. Important to assess protective parts and work with them first.\n  2. Develop a direct relationship with the part.\n  3. May need to negotiate pace of work -- give the part an opportunity to talk about concerns.\n  4. Work out a system for the part to let you know when things are moving too fast.\n  5. Respect the concerns of the part.\n* Non-imaging techniques\n  1. Assessing internal dialogue\n  2. Using the IFS language\n  3. Location/sense of a part in the body\n  4. Diagrams -- relationships among parts\n  5. Journaling\n  6. Direct access:\n     + Therapist to parts\n     + Self to parts\n     + Part to part\n* Imaging\n  1. Room technique\n  2. Mountain or path exercise\n  3. Going back in time with a part, then \"unburdening\"\n  4. Bringing parts into the present -- \"retrieval\"\n  5. Future imaging\n  6. Working with more than one part\n  7. Confronting abuse/significant others\n  8. Horizon/healing place\n  9. Use of light\n* Concept of Blending: keeping the feelings of the part from overwhelming the Self\n  1. Working with the Self to understand why/how not to blend\n  2. Working with the part to understand why/how not to blend\n* Working with young children\n  1. Assess developmental level of child and whether need to be concrete or able to use imaging techniques\n  2. Be creative, use modalities comfortable to child -- art, play techniques\n  3. Children respond well to techniques that externalize parts and then involve interacting with the parts, such as sandtray, puppets, and so on.\n\n**IX. WORKING WITH FAMILIES**\n\n* Introduce IFS language (power of IFS language vs. monolithic language)\n  1. Language is powerful in changing sequences.\n  2. Language frees people from seeing themselves (and others) in extreme ways.\n* Looking for parts that are activated in session.\n  1. Identifying sequences (both internal and external)\n  2. Selves working together to keep extreme parts of each family member from interfering\n* Enactments\n  1. Set up enactments of family.\n  2. Set up enactments of sequences/relationship among parts of individual family members.\n* Work with one family member while others watch.\n  1. Establish safety: Family members not to analyze parts outside of session\n  2. Contract not to talk about others' parts; can talk about own parts\n  3. No matter what others are doing, individual always responsible for own parts\n  4. Ask for reactions of others who are watching.\n  5. Try to alternate among family members.\n* Working with one member outside of family sessions\n  1. Emphasize taking responsibility for own parts and help practice accessing Self.\n* General frame of Selves working together to keep extreme parts of each family member from interfering\n\n**X. CONSTRAINTS TO THE WORK**\n\n* Therapist's parts (rational/scientific, approval, worrier, protective)\n* Protective parts of client\n* Protective parts of other family members\n* External system unsupportive or abusive\n\n**XI. COMMON THERAPIST MISTAKES**\n\n* Working with exile before system is ready.\n* Therapist assumes he/she is talking to person's Self when is talking to a part.\n* Therapist thinks Self is doing the work, but it's really a part.\n\n**XII. TROUBLESHOOTING PROBLEMS**\n\n* Helping Self to distance from/unblend from parts\n* Dealing with extreme parts\n\n**XIII. STRENGTHS OF THE MODEL**\n\n* Focuses on strengths: the undamaged core of the Self, the ability of parts to shift into positive roles\n* IFS language provides a way to look at oneself and others differently.\n* Language encourages self-disclosure and taking responsibility for behavior.\n* IFS language is powerful.\n* Provides a way to work with \"resistance\" and denial\n* Ecological understanding of entire therapy system, including therapist\n* Respect for individual's experience of the problem\n  1. Clients provide the material -- the therapist doesn't have to have all the ideas.\n  2. Therapist looks at client's Self as \"co-therapist\" and trusts the wisdom of the internal system.\n\n* [Home](/)\n* [About Us](/about-us)\n  + [Richard C. Schwartz, Ph.D.](/about-us/richard-c-schwartz-phd)\n    - [Bio and Appearances](/about-us/richard-c-schwartz-phd \"Richard Schwartz Appearances\")\n  + [Administrative
